Introduction\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p2\"\u003EAs sessile organisms, plants are constantly exposed to a wide variety of stress factors, such as desiccation, environmental pollution, temperature changes, and UV radiation. Ultraviolet radiation is a part of the nonionizing radiation region of the electromagnetic spectrum and comprises about 9% of the emitted solar radiation; according to the ISO 21348 standard, it is divided into three types: UV-C (200&ndash;280&nbsp;nm), UV-B (280&ndash;315&nbsp;nm), and UV-A (315&ndash;400&nbsp;nm) [\u003Ca href=\"#B1\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E1\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B2\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E2\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p3\"\u003EThe ozone layer (O\u003Csup\u003E3\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) efficiently filters much of the shortwave UV radiation (UV-C). However, this absorption decreases rapidly for radiation with wavelengths greater than 280&nbsp;nm, reaching a rate of 0% absorption for wavelengths greater than 330&nbsp;nm. Factors, such as elevation above sea level, cloud cover ground reflectance, geographic latitude, and ozone gradient and can affect the amount of UV-B and UV-A radiation that reaches the Earth&rsquo;s surface [\u003Ca href=\"#B3\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E3\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p4\"\u003EIn normal conditions, the ozone layer filters around 80% of UV-B radiation, but human activities have caused a decrease in the stratospheric ozone concentration through the emission of compounds such as chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs), carbon tetrachloride (CCl\u003Csub\u003E4\u003C\u002Fsub\u003E) and hydrochlorofluorocarbons (HCFCs). Therefore, UV-C radiation and an increased percentage of UV-B radiation can pass through [\u003Ca href=\"#B3\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E3\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B4\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E4\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p5\"\u003EAlthough UV radiation is a minor fraction of solar energy that reaches the earth&rsquo;s surface, it significantly affects plants. UV-B radiation affects important biomolecules directly, including nucleic acids and proteins; these molecules absorb UV radiation easily when presenting &pi; electrons, and this absorption can lead to metabolic, biochemical, and morphological alterations, as well as alterations in the genetic material [\u003Ca href=\"#B5\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E5\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B6\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E6\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UV-A radiation produces similar effects, although they are part of the constitutive regulation of plant metabolic and morphological processes, such as photosynthesis, biomass production, and synthesis of pigments and antioxidant compounds [\u003Ca href=\"#B7\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E7\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p6\"\u003ESince the discovery of the thinning of the ozone layer, the consequent penetration of UV-B radiation into the atmosphere and its undisputable contribution to global warming of the planet, the effects of UV radiation on plants have been closely studied. Plants can use sunlight not only as a source of energy to produce carbon compounds but also as a source of environmental information; that is, they can detect it as a signal and trigger different systemic responses related to photosynthesis, phototropism, photoperiodicity, and photomorphogenesis. These same processes can be affected by the abnormal incidence of UV radiation in the atmosphere; therefore, the impact of its damage has been studied in recent decades [\u003Ca href=\"#B8\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E8\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. This assessment has led to the creation of initiatives such as the Montreal Protocol, which aims at mitigating the negative effects of climate change-derived increased UV exposure through international policies [\u003Ca href=\"#B9\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E9\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p7\"\u003EIn addition, the analysis of the causes of the morphological alterations shown by plants under UV light stress is difficult because they can be affected simultaneously by other environmental factors such as temperature, salinity, or drought, which together can modify development at the cellular level. The objective of this chapter is to describe the effects of UV radiation on different biochemical, morphological, and genetic processes in plants.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_2\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E2. Morphological alterations\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p8\"\u003EPhotomorphogenesis (light-regulated plant development) in the presence of UV light has been extensively studied [\u003Ca href=\"#B10\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E10\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Plants of several species modify the development of their organs in the presence of UV light; for example, the length of the stems tends to shorten, although they form a greater number of axillary buds, while the roots tend to be longer and more abundant, akin to the development of plants that grow in conditions of low light radiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B11\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E11\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p9\"\u003EOne of the stages of plant development most susceptible to the incidence of light is germination, which is also greatly affected by UV-B radiation. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EArabidopsis thaliana\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E seedlings irradiated with UV light, the growth of the hypocotyl was slower [\u003Ca href=\"#B12\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E12\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] compared to seedlings germinating under normal conditions; even the growth of the hypocotyl is lower in etiolated plants developed in the shade but irradiated with UV light [\u003Ca href=\"#B13\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E13\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. On the contrary, in this same species, it has been observed that, under these conditions, the cotyledons tend to expand, even with short periods of UV light exposure [\u003Ca href=\"#B14\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E14\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p10\"\u003ELeaves also modify their structure, tending to decrease their surface area and increase their thickness in many broadleaf plant species that have been tested for their response to UV light. Apparently, this change in morphology depends on the imbalance between cell proliferation and elongation among the different leaf tissues, which can cause a decrease in leaf area, abnormal thickening, or rolling, resulting in slow plant development [\u003Ca href=\"#B15\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E15\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. While searching for modifications at the cellular level that explain the alterations in the morphology of plants under UV stress, Krasilenko et al. demonstrated in 2013 [\u003Ca href=\"#B16\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E16\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] that UV radiation can cause depolymerization or fragmentation of microtubules in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EA. thaliana\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E cells, causing the reorganization of the cytoskeleton and the cell in general, so that elongation and cell division are reduced, resulting in the formation of shorter leaves, which affects the development and the complete morphology of the plant.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p11\"\u003EIt is currently accepted that some plant species avoid excess light radiation by forming a waxy cuticle on the epidermis. Exposure to UV radiation-induced deposition of wax in plants of species, such as \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECoffea arabica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECoffea canephora\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EHordeum vulgare\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECucumis sativus,\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E and \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPhaseolus vulgaris\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, which results in an increase in the thickness of the cuticle. Additionally, molecules such as phenolic acids and flavonoids can accumulate in the cuticle, functioning as photoprotectors against UV light or as UV light attenuators, respectively [\u003Ca href=\"#B17\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E17\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p12\"\u003EStomata, the structures where gas exchange occurs, are also affected by the presence of UV light. High UV irradiation causes loss of stomatal opening and closing control in response to environmental stimuli, apparently due to an altered guard cell conductance. Since the stomatic function is vital for CO\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsub\u003E fixation in the light-independent reactions of photosynthesis, its deregulation can deeply affect plant development and physiology [\u003Ca href=\"#B18\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E18\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p13\"\u003EUV light plays an important role in plant development, but extreme exposure can be detrimental. Unable to relocate, plants must balance the positive and negative effects of UV radiation mostly through intracellular mechanisms, as described in the following sections.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_3\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E3. Photosynthetic alterations\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p14\"\u003EPhotosynthesis is a light-dependent process, so it is almost inevitable that it be affected by the presence of UV radiation. There are several reports about the damage caused by UV radiation in specific sites of the photosynthetic apparatus of green plants (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"fig\"\u003EFigure 1A\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) [\u003Ca href=\"#B17\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E17\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Much of the damage is caused by the enhanced production of reactive oxygen species (ROS) that are involved in UV-induced responses, both as signaling agents within normal cellular processes and as damaging agents. ROS can cause damage to the proteins that make up the light-harvesting complexes of the photosystems or to those found in the protein complexes where the electron carriers of photosynthesis are concentrated, their accumulation is even known to cause the destruction of ribulose bisphosphate carboxylase\u002Foxygenase [\u003Ca href=\"#B19\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E19\u003C\u002Fa\u003E], and, therefore, a decrease in atmospheric carbon fixation and plant biomass occurs. Another important damage caused by ROS is the oxidation of fatty acids in the membranes, which, in combination with peroxidation and photooxidation because of UV light, breaks the essential integrity of the thylakoid membranes in the chloroplast, generating alterations in the organization of the membrane-embedded photosynthetic complexes, decreasing their photosynthetic capacity [\u003Ca href=\"#B20\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E20\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"media-panel\" id=\"F1\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"media\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"\u002F\u002Fcdnintech.com\u002Fmedia\u002Fchapter\u002F85608\u002F1690965638\u002Fmedia\u002FF1.png\" class=\"figure-link\" alt=\"\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cfigcaption class=\"caption\"\u003E\u003Ch4\u003EFigure 1.\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cp xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\" id=\"p15\"\u003EEffects of UV-B light on plants and alterations caused by UV-B radiation in photosynthetic metabolism (A) and secondary metabolism (B).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigcaption\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p16\"\u003EUltraviolet light also causes damage to plant proteins; in fact, one of the effects on photosynthesis is the damage, it exerts on the enzymes that synthesize pigments such as chlorophylls [\u003Ca href=\"#B21\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E21\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In addition, pigments are also degraded by UV light, especially chlorophyll b and carotenoids, so exposure to this type of radiation can cause an imbalance in the proportion of pigments, with the consequent alteration of the photosynthetic apparatus, as has been observed recently in maize. After being exposed to UV radiation for 19&nbsp;days, fluorescence and chlorophyll concentration decreased in several maize lines, although in different proportions in a line-dependent manner [\u003Ca href=\"#B22\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E22\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p17\"\u003ESeveral elements at Photosystem II, the site where photosynthesis begins, are sensitive to UV radiation. This complex is formed by the association of pigments and proteins, and many of these proteins are part of electron transport centers; therefore, their alteration or degradation affects the electron transport chain of photosynthesis, reducing their levels under UV light stress. In an elegant work, Ihle [\u003Ca href=\"#B23\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E23\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] reported that proteins D1 and D2, which are found in the reaction center of photosystem II, are especially susceptible even to low intensities of UV radiation (1&nbsp;&mu;mol&nbsp;m\u003Csup\u003E&minus;2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E&nbsp;s\u003Csup\u003E&minus;1\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) [\u003Ca href=\"#B24\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E24\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The degradation of proteins D1 and D2 adds to the alteration of the manganese (Mn) oxidizing group of water, which together cause the loss of function of the reaction center and, therefore, the inhibition of electron transport [\u003Ca href=\"#B25\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E25\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Damage at the beginning of the electron transport chain of photosynthesis makes it difficult to investigate downstream transporters; however, some reports indicate the change in the ratio of photosystems II and I due to the decrease in absorption at 700&nbsp;nm&mdash;absorbed by Photosystem I&mdash;observed after prolonged exposure to UV light [\u003Ca href=\"#B26\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E26\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p18\"\u003EPlants are highly susceptible to the presence of ultraviolet light. Through research over the past four decades, it has been possible to discover the mechanisms related to damage in plant morphology, development, and metabolism. However, many questions remain to be investigated until the problem of the penetration of UV radiation into the atmosphere is resolved.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_4\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E4. Oxidative stress by UV light induction\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p19\"\u003EUltraviolet radiation is an important stress in plants that elicits protective mechanisms such as the accumulation of secondary metabolites in the cell (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"fig\"\u003EFigure 1B\u003C\u002Fa\u003E) [\u003Ca href=\"#B27\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E27\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B28\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E28\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B29\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E29\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] and an increase in leaf thickness [\u003Ca href=\"#B30\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E30\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Interestingly, UV radiation is a hormetic stimulus, that is severe exposure is harmful, but exposure to lower sub-acute levels can stimulate protective mechanisms [\u003Ca href=\"#B31\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E31\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Consequently, plants can become resilient to UV after repeated exposure [\u003Ca href=\"#B32\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E32\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p20\"\u003EThe changes in the secondary metabolism of plants from all taxa under exposure to UV radiation have been widely documented. For instance, in the moss \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPohlia nutans\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, UV-B radiation enhanced flavone biosynthesis through increasing type I flavone synthase activity [\u003Ca href=\"#B33\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E33\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ETaxus cuspidate\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, UV-B radiation (3&nbsp;W\u002Fm\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) provoked the accumulation of toxoids and flavonoids [\u003Ca href=\"#B34\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E34\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Also, the flavonoid contents in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EScutellaria baicalensis\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E reached the maximum concentration (41.86&nbsp;mg\u002Fg\u003Csup\u003E&minus;1\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) after seven days under UV-A radiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B35\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E35\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPisum sativum\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E leaves, exposure to UV-B radiation increased the nicotinamide and trigonelline content; the nicotinamide induction is an oxidative stress reaction [\u003Ca href=\"#B36\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E36\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In an analysis performed on two different ecotypes of the \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPaubrasilia echinata\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E tree, it was shown that UV-B radiation inhibited stem growth, biomass accumulation, CO\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsub\u003E assimilation, and photochemical efficiency in a shade-tolerant ecotype inhibition; in contrast, a sun-tolerant ecotype showed a positive response: UV-B increased flavonoids, lignin, and antioxidant properties, but reduced cell respiration [\u003Ca href=\"#B37\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E37\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPinus radiata,\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E UV radiation provoked an early response reducing photosystem activity and accumulation of photoprotectors; even the primary metabolism was rearranged to minimize ROS production, also the isoprenoids compounds like carotenoids, tocopherols, phytol, and gibberellins were decreased [\u003Ca href=\"#B38\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E38\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Under exposure to UV-B radiation followed by dark treatment, the number of flavonoids and coumarins in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EClematis terniflora\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E increased significantly; while proteins related to photorespiration, the tricarboxylic acid cycle, and mitochondrial permeability showed differential expression profiles, indicating that UV-B radiation induces a reduction in energy consumption and maintains energy balance [\u003Ca href=\"#B39\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E39\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ENymphoides humboldtiana\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E increased antioxidant activity and production of flavonoids like phloroglucinol, chlorogenic acid, epicatechin, quercetin, and ferulic acid after 13&nbsp;days of exposition of UV-B radiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B40\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E40\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EColobanthus quitensis\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E under UV-B radiation increased the biosynthesis of flavonoids, particularly flavone C-glycosides, metabolites located within the most metabolically active cells [\u003Ca href=\"#B41\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E41\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EMelisa officinalis\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E showed changes in the glycolysis and phenylpropanoid pathway under UV radiation stress with differential recovery times [\u003Ca href=\"#B28\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E28\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p21\"\u003EStudies on algae have shown similar mechanisms, as expected by their phylogenetical relation to plants. For example, UV-B radiation-induced ROS production in peroxisomes and chloroplasts in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EUlva prolifera\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E provokes irreversible damage under 5&nbsp;W&nbsp;m\u003Csup\u003E&minus;2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E [\u003Ca href=\"#B42\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E42\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EChlamydomonas reinhardtii,\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E UV-C radiation stress increased ROS levels and production of antioxidant polyphenols, a phenolic including caffeic acid, cinnamic acid, coumaric acid, salicylic acid, and protocatechuic acid, among others [\u003Ca href=\"#B43\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E43\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p22\"\u003EA notable example among plant-derived compounds is the alkaloid mimosine, present in the seedlings of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ELeucaena leucocephala\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E spp. Glabrata is particularly interesting due to its therapeutic uses as anti-cancer, antifungal, and antimicrobial, which increase its economic interest. Acute UV-C exposure of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EL. leucocephala\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E seedlings induced a strong accumulation of mimosine, which could be implicated in general oxidative stress modulation [\u003Ca href=\"#B44\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E44\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p23\"\u003EThe effect of UV-radiation stress has also been extensively studied in plants used in traditional medicine. For example, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EMorus alba\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, used in traditional Chinese medicine, reduces its growth and secondary metabolism after exposure to UV-B [\u003Ca href=\"#B45\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E45\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EGingko biloba\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E leaves, after long-term exposure to UV-B radiation, increase flavonoids biosynthesis, and these are beneficial as therapeutic active ingredients [\u003Ca href=\"#B29\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E29\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Two different species of the Chinese herb Astragalus modified their secondary metabolite production under UV-B radiation; \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EA. mebranaceus\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E produced increased hydroxycinnamic acid derivates, while \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EAstragalus mongholicus\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E accumulated myricitrin and isoflavones, showing different tolerance to UV-B stress [\u003Ca href=\"#B30\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E30\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The flowers of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ELonicera japonica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E are used as a medicinal herb in Asian countries. Under UV radiation, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EL. japonica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E increases the levels of oxidative pentose phosphate and secondary metabolites such as secologanic acid, secoxyloganin, and isochlorogenic acid [\u003Ca href=\"#B46\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E46\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EAdhatoda vasica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, also used in Asiatic traditional medicine, UV-B radiation (7.2 kj m\u003Csup\u003E&minus;2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E day\u003Csup\u003E&minus;1\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) induces a reduction of superoxide radical production while increasing hydrogen peroxide production [\u003Ca href=\"#B47\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E47\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Finally, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECentella asiatica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, used in Asian and African traditional medicine, accumulated saponins and epidermal flavonols under UV-B radiation in younger leaves with high levels of saponins; in contrast, in older leaves, sapogenins were the most abundant metabolites [\u003Ca href=\"#B48\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E48\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p24\"\u003EAs shown in these studies, UV light has forced algae, bryophytes, and plants to modify their metabolism&mdash;particularly the secondary metabolism&mdash;to increase their ecological success rate. However, this also has important consequences for plants of commercial interest, as seen below.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_5\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E5. UV radiation as functional quality of plant foods of commercial interest\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p25\"\u003EHistorically, economically important plants have been exhaustively studied; recent studies have focused particularly on UV light stress, searching for alterations in organoleptic properties and secondary metabolism. In modern horticulture, plants of economic interest have been irradiated with UV light during the flowering\u002Ffruiting period, with the purpose of stimulating oxidative stress pathways as well as antioxidant production [\u003Ca href=\"#B49\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E49\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In tomato juice production, the stress caused by UV radiation in plants decreased pectolytic enzymes, improving and preserving tomato characteristics for a longer period of time [\u003Ca href=\"#B50\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E50\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Also, in a tomato cultivar, UV-A and B radiation produced higher ripening synchronization and smaller fruits. Exposure to UV-A radiation-induced accumulation of phenolics and flavonoids, making these fruits more appealing to consumers [\u003Ca href=\"#B51\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E51\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Furthermore, in tomato seedlings under UV-B radiation, carotenoid content increased as well as antioxidant enzyme activities [\u003Ca href=\"#B52\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E52\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p26\"\u003EAnother plant of great economic importance is soybean (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EGlycinine max\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E), which increases the isoflavone content of the sprouts under UV radiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B53\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E53\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In soybean seedlings, nitric oxide is induced as a protection against UV-B stress [\u003Ca href=\"#B54\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E54\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Meanwhile, on germinated soybean, UV-B radiation increased the contents of linoleic acid and erucic acid content, as well as isoflavones, phenolic acids, vitamin C, folate, and chlorophyll, improving nutritional and functional qualities [\u003Ca href=\"#B55\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E55\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Conversely, excessive UV-B exposure damaged cells and decreased the amount of isoflavones within them [\u003Ca href=\"#B56\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E56\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In cultured soybean, UV-C radiation increased the amount of genistein-O-glucoside and genistein-O-glucosyl-malonate, suggesting \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Ein vitro\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E culture to obtain a high level of metabolites [\u003Ca href=\"#B57\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E57\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Moreover, in germinated soybean under UV-B radiation, total protein content and endogenous H\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsub\u003EO\u003Csub\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsub\u003E were increased [\u003Ca href=\"#B58\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E58\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p27\"\u003ECereals and ornate flowers also have responses to UV radiation. Wheat seedlings under UV stress showed an increase of phenylalanine ammonia-lyase only in the roots, indicating that UV-B radiation has a positive or negative impact, depending on the type of secondary abiotic stress factor observable in the production of phenolic compounds [\u003Ca href=\"#B59\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E59\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Also, germinated wheat under UV-B radiation increased phenols, ferulic acid, and coumaric acid. Exogenous Ca\u003Csup\u003E2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E+ positively affected free and bound phenolic accumulations [\u003Ca href=\"#B60\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E60\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In amaranth (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EAmaranthus cruentus\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E L.), UV-C radiation improved postharvest quality by increasing levels of quercetin, kaempferol, copene, lutein, &beta;-carotene, and caffeic acid derivates [\u003Ca href=\"#B27\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E27\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In lily bulbs, UV-C radiation increased total phenolic content and antioxidant activity, indicating that UV-C radiation is a safe alternative for processing lily bulbs in storage [\u003Ca href=\"#B61\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E61\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p28\"\u003ELikewise, the effects of UV radiation have been studied in economically important herbs. In spinach cultivars, UV-C induced a hormetic effect that increased total phenolic compounds and reduced the presence of the parasite fungi \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EAlternaria\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E ssp. in the crops [\u003Ca href=\"#B62\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E62\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In barley seedlings, UV-B radiation up-regulated enzymatic activity, resulting in the accumulation of phenolic acids [\u003Ca href=\"#B63\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E63\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EMentha aquatic\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E responded to UV-B radiation on a morphological level, increasing glandular trichomes, and on a biochemical level, increasing oxidative metabolism and overexpressing genes implicated in terpene biosynthesis, particularly volatile oils as camphene, &beta;-pinene, and germacrene [\u003Ca href=\"#B64\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E64\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In wounded carrots under UV-A and C radiation, ROS increased, acting as a signal for ethylene synthesis, which activated the synthesis of jasmonic acid leading to the accumulation of phenolic compounds [\u003Ca href=\"#B65\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E65\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In fresh-cut carrots, UV-C doses inhibited ascorbic acid, total carotenoid, respiration, total phenols, lignin, malondialdehyde, and ethylene production; all data collected indicated extended shelf-life and overall quality maintenance [\u003Ca href=\"#B66\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E66\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In parsley, UV-C doses resulted in an increase of antioxidants such as phenylpropanoid and phenolic compounds, as well as enzymes involved in the synthesis of phenylpropanoid [\u003Ca href=\"#B49\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E49\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The effect of UV radiation induces the production of 6&Prime;-0-malonylapiin, which is a flavone glycoside, as well as the 12-oxo-phytodienoic acid [\u003Ca href=\"#B67\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E67\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UV-B radiation (1.5&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;m\u003Csup\u003E&minus;2\u003C\u002Fsup\u003E) maintained the color of broccoli florets during storage, and induced glucosinolates and hydroxyl-cinnamates, raising their antioxidant properties. These findings suggested that UV-B radiation is likely to induce the indole glucosinolate pathway [\u003Ca href=\"#B31\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E31\u003C\u002Fa\u003E], maintaining the quality of broccoli florets in low-temperature storage [\u003Ca href=\"#B68\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E68\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p29\"\u003EFruits are also of economic interest and respond differentially to UV. Grape berries (Jumeigui variety) decreased sugar content under UV-C, promoting the accumulation of stilbenes and some flavonoids [\u003Ca href=\"#B69\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E69\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In contrast, berry clusters (red table emperor) under UV-A and B radiation decreased the amount of quercetin 3-O-glucoside and quercetin 3-O-glucuronide, suggesting that UV radiation induces postharvest changes in phenolic metabolites [\u003Ca href=\"#B70\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E70\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In fresh-cut strawberries, UV-C increased phenolic compounds, anthocyanin, cyanidin 3-glucoside, pelargonidin 3-glucoside, and cyanidin 3-glucoside-succinate, activating the phenylpropanoid pathway, thus improving antioxidant capacity without losing fruit quality [\u003Ca href=\"#B71\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E71\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In two blueberry cultivars (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EVaccinium corymbosum\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E), exposure to UV radiation showed that the amount of phenylpropanoid compounds was higher in the Legacy cultivar than in the Bluegold cultivar, which indicates that UV-B acclimation is different between cultivars [\u003Ca href=\"#B72\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E72\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Moreover, in highbush blueberry leaves (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EV. corymbosum\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E L. cv. Brigitta and Bluegold), photosynthesis decreased in the Bluegold variety under UV-B radiation; in contrast, the Brigitta variety increased the photosynthesis rate as well as antioxidant activity [\u003Ca href=\"#B73\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E73\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In fragrant pear, postharvest UV-C radiation controlled blackhead disease through chitinase, &beta;-1,3-glucanase, peroxidase, superoxide dismutase, catalase, ascorbate peroxidase, and phenylalanine ammonia-lyase [\u003Ca href=\"#B74\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E74\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In nectarine, UV-C radiation induced an increase in anthocyanin biosynthesis and promoted the antioxidant system, stimulating the phenyl propane pathway. Together, these compounds exerted antifungal action against \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ER. stolonifera\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E [\u003Ca href=\"#B75\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E75\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In young leaves of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EVitis vinifera\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, low UV-B radiation increase sitosterol, stigmasterol, and lupeol, probably as an acclimation response. In contrast, diterpenes, tocopherol, phytol, E-nerolidol, monoterpenes as careen, &alpha;-pinene, and terpinolene were present in high amounts in mature leaves; these results showed that the synthesis of terpenes is an adaptive response to UV-B radiation stress [\u003Ca href=\"#B76\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E76\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In postharvest lemon fruits after UV-B radiation, phenolic compounds increased in flavedo, indicating that lemon peel modifies enzymatic activities involved in sucrose metabolism [\u003Ca href=\"#B77\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E77\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p30\"\u003EFurthermore, in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EOlea europaea\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, UV-B radiation increases secoiridoids and 2&Prime;-methoxyoleuropein metabolites, while decreasing oleuropein as an antioxidant defense against UV [\u003Ca href=\"#B78\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E78\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The peach (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EPrunus persica\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E) diminishes the synthesis of anthocyanins and phenolic compounds under UV-B exposure, but after 36&nbsp;h, it increases anthocyanins, cyanidin, and delphinidin compounds [\u003Ca href=\"#B79\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E79\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In Luffa seedlings, the oxylipins such as methyl jasmonate and 12-Oxo-phytodienoic acid mitigated the UV-B stress via improved photosynthetic and nitrogen metabolism, respectively [\u003Ca href=\"#B80\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E80\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p31\"\u003EEven economically important algae respond to UV radiation. In several Spirulina species, mild stress by UV-B radiation has been useful in increasing physiological and nutritional competencies in growth, rendering UV radiation useful in producing this functional food [\u003Ca href=\"#B81\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E81\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p32\"\u003EAlthough most of the above-mentioned economically important species appeared to benefit from UV exposure, it has been detrimental to some species. Rice (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EO. sativa\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E) plants treated with UV-C had less palatability and were easily infested by the weevil \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ESitophilus oryzae\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E, which provoked lower consumer acceptance and purchase intention [\u003Ca href=\"#B82\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E82\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Also, sweet cherry fruits under UV-C radiation diminished respiration, but increased rhamnose, mannose, galactarate, threonate, and aspartate contents [\u003Ca href=\"#B83\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E83\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p33\"\u003EThis evidence highlights the importance of studying UV stress in plants of economic interest, as it can lead to higher yields and thus higher profits. However, care must be taken before implementing UV irradiation as a production-boosting resource because some species might be impacted negatively, as evidenced by the effects of the increased exposure to UV derived from climate change. Plant litter decomposition, especially in regions with low annual rainfall and reduction of photosynthetically active radiation (PAR), further strain crop production [\u003Ca href=\"#B84\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E84\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_6\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E6. Genetic response\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p34\"\u003ESeveral studies have focused on changes in gene expression in plants exposed to long- and short-wave UV-B radiation to identify the cellular components that regulate response to UV [\u003Ca href=\"#B85\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E85\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The results showed that UV-B radiation triggers cell growth and morphogenesis pathways [\u003Ca href=\"#B86\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E86\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UV-B response signals are also transmitted from cell to cell and are usually organ-specific [\u003Ca href=\"#B87\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E87\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p35\"\u003EGenetic approaches for phenotypic responses to UV-B are based on models of increased tolerance or aberrant responses (e.g., changes in hypocotyl growth) to UV-B irradiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B86\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E86\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Transcriptomic analysis from \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EArabidopsis\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E seedlings exposed to different UV-B radiation intensities showed that more than 20% of the genes that modified their expression are transcription factors [\u003Ca href=\"#B85\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E85\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. These approaches allowed the identification of mutants that lacked or overexpressed photoprotective compounds or inhibited hypocotyl growth in response to UV-B [\u003Ca href=\"#B14\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E14\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B88\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E88\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p36\"\u003EUV-B radiation induces changes in the expression of genes that affect growth and development, as seen in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EUV-B light insensitive\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Euli\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E) mutant plants, which present reduced hypocotyl growth relative to wild-type after UV-B exposure. Also, UV-B affects \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Echalcon synthase (CHS)\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E expression [\u003Ca href=\"#B14\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E14\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]; low levels of irradiation activate this gene, which is key in the biosynthesis of phenylpropanoids [\u003Ca href=\"#B89\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E89\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECHS,\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E along with transcription factors, allows plants to protect themselves against UV-B.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p37\"\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ELONG HYPOCOTYL5 (HY5)\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E is a bZIP transcription factor that regulates morphogenesis in response to UV-B. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EHY5\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E gene expression is a component of the UV-B-induced signaling network. Transcriptomic analysis in \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EArabidopsis thaliana\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E showed the importance of HY5-dependent regulation in response to low-level UV-B irradiation [\u003Ca href=\"#B85\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E85\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. If HY5 is lost, transcriptional induction of the UV-B response genes is impaired [\u003Ca href=\"#B86\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E86\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] and cells undergo programmed death [\u003Ca href=\"#B90\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E90\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p38\"\u003EHY5 is a light-induced transcription factor required for many light-responsive genes; in the dark, it is degraded by the proteasome [\u003Ca href=\"#B91\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E91\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. This transcription factor is key for phytochrome and cryptochrome regulation networks [\u003Ca href=\"#B85\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E85\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B92\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E92\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B93\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E93\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. So, it seems that HY5 does not respond to UV-B radiation exclusively, which opens the door for research on other components that specifically drive plants&rsquo; response to UV-B.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p39\"\u003ESeveral genes are induced by UV-B independently from traditional photoreceptors, such as phytochromes and cryptochromes, through the activity of the LONG HYPOCOTYL5 (HY5) transcription factor [\u003Ca href=\"#B85\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E85\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. This independence suggests that there must be a specific UV-B photoreceptor that activates HY5; however, the identity of this putative element is still unclear [\u003Ca href=\"#B94\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E94\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p40\"\u003ECONSTITUTIVELY PHOTOMORPHOGENIC1 (COP1) is an E3 ubiquitin ligase that participates in the UV-B response [\u003Ca href=\"#B95\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E95\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. COP1 has three functional domains, a RING finger (ligase activity), a coiled-coil for dimerization, and a WD40 repeat domain with binding activity [\u003Ca href=\"#B93\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E93\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. COP1 targets bZIP transcription factors and is required to activate \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EHY5\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E gene expression. Both proteins are localized in the nucleus and regulate photomorphogenesis under UV-B conditions in a specialized pathway [\u003Ca href=\"#B95\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E95\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. COP1 was identified as a photomorphogenesis repressor in darkness and light [\u003Ca href=\"#B93\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E93\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Visible light inactivated COP1 and separated it from HY5 [\u003Ca href=\"#B93\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E93\u003C\u002Fa\u003E], allowing HY5 stabilization and, therefore, activation of light-responsive genes [\u003Ca href=\"#B91\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E91\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B97\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E97\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] through the interaction of phytochromes and cryptochromes [\u003Ca href=\"#B98\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E98\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B99\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E99\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Phytochromes and cryptochromes interact with the SUPPRESOR OF PHYTOCHROME A (SPA) proteins, causing light-dependent COP1 inactivation. COP1 response to UV-B radiation is independent of the SPA proteins [\u003Ca href=\"#B95\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E95\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B100\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E100\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]; rather, COP1 responds to UV-B through the interaction with the UV RESPONSE LOCUS 8 (UVR8) protein [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p41\"\u003EUVR8 is a seven-bladed b-propeller protein that forms a homodimer in its inactive state [\u003Ca href=\"#B101\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E101\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B102\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E102\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] and is capable of UVR-B perception [\u003Ca href=\"#B103\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E103\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B104\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E104\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. In contrast to other photoreceptors (phytochrome and cryptochrome), UVR8 does not employ a bound chromophore; instead, it uses a tryptophan residue localized in the b-propeller blade [\u003Ca href=\"#B101\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E101\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B102\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E102\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B105\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E105\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. Upon UV-B absorption, the UVR8 dimer destabilizes and the monomeric form interacts with COP1 [\u003Ca href=\"#B104\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E104\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. The UVR8-COP1 heterodimer activates the transcription factor HY5, consequently activating downstream genes that are implied in metabolic and morphological alterations [\u003Ca href=\"#B104\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E104\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B106\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E106\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]; this mechanism activates UV-B acclimation and tolerance [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UVR8 is usually located in the cytoplasm, while COP1 is in the nuclear bodies of hypocotyl cells. When plants are irradiated with UV-B, UVR8 translocates to the nucleus [\u003Ca href=\"#B107\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E107\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] and colocalizes to the COP1-rich nuclear bodies [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. After UV-B exposition, the UVR8 dimer COP1 prevents HY5 degradation, so that HY5 can exert its transcriptional activation function [\u003Ca href=\"#B108\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E108\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p42\"\u003EIn Arabidopsis plants, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Euvr8\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E mutants do not respond when grown under UV-B radiation; they lack a photomorphogenic signal and therefore do not display the damage usually found in wild-type plants [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UV-B-induced gene expression is important for UV acclimation and survival. When \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Eurv8\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E and \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Ecop1\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E mutants are initially grown in weak UV-B exposure and later moved to high UV-B irradiance, the mutants do not show an acclimation effect. When exposed to a natural spectral balance, the \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003Euvr8\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E mutant shows leaf damage. Also, \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EHY5\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E or \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECHS\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E gene expression is undetectable in both mutants. Consequently, the interaction between COP1 and UVR8 proteins is required for the regulation of UV-B response and confers UV-B protection. On the other hand, overexpression of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EUVR8\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E leads to UV-B photomorphogenic hypersensitivity, presenting inhibition of hypocotyl growth, activation of \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EHY5\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E and \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ECHS\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E gene, and accumulation of anthocyanin [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p43\"\u003ECOP1 is related to the repression of photomorphogenesis but it seems that UVR8 provides UV-B-specific signaling and that the interaction COP1-UVR8 occurs within minutes [\u003Ca href=\"#B96\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E96\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. UVR8 is reverted to homodimer (inactive form) through the REPRESSOR OF UV-B PHOTOMORPHOGENESIS proteins (RUP1 and 2). RUP1 and RUP2 are two highly related WD40-repeat proteins that interact directly with UVR8 promoting its homodimerization, thus acting act as negative regulators [\u003Ca href=\"#B104\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E104\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B108\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E108\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"#B109\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E109\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]. \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ERUP 1\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E and \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003ERUP 2\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E are induced by UV-B but act downstream of UVR8-COP1 forming a negative feedback loop that balances UV-B defense [\u003Ca href=\"#B109\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"bibr\"\u003E109\u003C\u002Fa\u003E] (\u003Ca href=\"#F2\" class=\"ref-link\" data-ref-style=\"fig\"\u003EFigure 2\u003C\u002Fa\u003E).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"media-panel\" id=\"F2\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"media\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"\u002F\u002Fcdnintech.com\u002Fmedia\u002Fchapter\u002F85608\u002F1690965638\u002Fmedia\u002FF2.png\" class=\"figure-link\" alt=\"\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cfigcaption class=\"caption\"\u003E\u003Ch4\u003EFigure 2.\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cp xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\" id=\"p44\"\u003EELONGATED HYPOCOTYL5 (HY5) transcription factor activation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigcaption\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p45\"\u003EInhibition of the transcription factor HY5 by binding with COP1 in the absence of UV-B light, the expression of the response genes remains inactive (left). In the presence of UV-B light, the monomeric form of UVR8 enters the nucleus, binds to COP1, and activates HY5 (right).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_7\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003E7. Conclusions\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p46\"\u003EThe effects that UV radiation causes on plants have been extensively investigated from different perspectives. Studies on alterations in photomorphogenesis, primary metabolism, particularly photosynthesis, secondary metabolism, or gene expression have been carried out with model plants such as \u003Cem\u003E\u003Citalic xmlns:mml=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1998\u002FMath\u002FMathML\" xmlns:xlink=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F1999\u002Fxlink\" xmlns:xsi=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.w3.org\u002F2001\u002FXMLSchema-instance\"\u003EArabidopsis thaliana\u003C\u002Fitalic\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E; however, several other aspects still need to be addressed. Current technologies, such as omics tools, allow for the study of plants in their natural environments, considering all their complexity, and will, undoubtedly, lead to a better understanding of the impact of UV radiation in plants, an important constituent of climate change. Looking into the future, an integral view of plant responses to UV radiation has broad applications in agriculture and conservation, while providing scientific foundations for upcoming international regulations.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"section\" id=\"sec_8\" data-lvl=\"1\"\u003E\u003Ch2 class=\"heading main-title\"\u003EAcknowledgments\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp id=\"p47\"\u003EConsejo Nacional de Ciencia y Tecnolog&iacute;a [CONACyT, Ciencia B&aacute;sica Grant No. A1-S-14605].\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\n","keywords":"UV radiation, secondary metabolites, oxidative stress, photomorphogenesis, photosynthesis, UV transcription factors","chapterPDFUrl":"https:\u002F\u002Fcdn.intechopen.com\u002Fpdfs\u002F85608.pdf","chapterXML":"https:\u002F\u002Fmts.intechopen.com\u002Fsource\u002Fxml\u002F85608.xml","webChapterXML":"s3:\u002F\u002Fintech-chapter-xmls\u002Fxmls-chapter\u002F85608\u002F1686941763\u002F","downloadPdfUrl":"\u002Fchapter\u002Fpdf-download\u002F85608","previewPdfUrl":"\u002Fchapter\u002Fpdf-preview\u002F85608","cdnMediaBaseUrl":"s3:\u002F\u002Fintech-cdn\u002Fmedia\u002Fchapter\u002F85608\u002F1690965638\u002F","totalDownloads":1591,"totalViews":12483,"totalCrossrefCites":5,"totalDimensionsCites":19,"dateSubmitted":"November 19th 2022","dateReviewed":"December 12th 2022","datePrePublished":"January 10th 2023","datePublished":"July 5th 2023","dateFinished":"January 10th 2023","readingETA":"0","abstract":"Ultraviolet radiation is a portion of the electromagnetic spectrum ranging from 10 to 400 nm, classified into three main categories: UV-A (320–400 nm), UV-B (280–320 nm), and UV-C (100–280 nm). The UV radiation from the sun that crosses the atmosphere and reaches the earth’s surface is composed largely of UV-A radiation (95%) and, to a lesser extent, UV-B (5%), which is normally filtered by stratospheric ozone. With the thinning of the ozone layer, UV-B radiation penetrates deeper into the earth’s surface, where it becomes dangerous due to its high energy content that acts at the molecular level, affecting the cycles of carbon, nitrogen, and other elements, thus, having a direct impact on global warming. On the other hand, UV radiation alters numerous essential organic compounds for living organisms. Since its discovery, it has been established that e UV-B causes alterations in plant development and metabolism, both primary and secondary. In this chapter, we summarize the current knowledge about the effects of UV radiation on the morphological, biochemical, and genetic processes in plants.","reviewType":"peer-reviewed","bibtexUrl":"\u002Fchapter\u002Fbibtex\u002F85608","risUrl":"\u002Fchapter\u002Fris\u002F85608","signatures":"María del Socorro Sánchez Correa, María el Rocío Reyero Saavedra, Edgar Antonio Estrella Parra, Erick Nolasco Ontiveros, José del Carmen Benítez Flores, Juan Gerardo Ortiz Montiel, Jorge Eduardo Campos Contreras, Eduardo López Urrutia, José Guillermo Ávila Acevedo, Gladys Edith Jiménez Nopala and Adriana Montserrat Espinosa González","isPublished":true,"isOnlineFirst":false,"isDeactivated":0,"isDeactivated2":0,"noAds":0,"licenceType":"CC BY 3.0","copyright":"© 2023 The Author(s).
